1400069742|5.0|If, like me, you've always been puzzled and almost ashamed at how long it took America to enter WWII then this is not a book to be missed. I've read brief and very dry explanations for why this happened. I've heard angry allegations of anti-Semitism and waffling on the part of the Roosevelt administration. But for a clear, well written and logical explanation of what exactly happened in those days when America hung back from entering the war, even as country after country fell to the Nazis, this has to be one of the best, easily approachable books written on the subject.<br /><br />The book begins with a beatific image of Charles Lindberg, American Hero and modern Galahad, slipping into the Smithsonian museum to simply look at his plane, The Spirit of St. Louis, as it hangs suspended from the ceiling of the main Hall. If all you know about Lindberg is that he flew this little plane cross-Atlantic and became a modern hero, then this book is going to be a surprise for you.<br /><br />As most things political, there was great complexity as to why America stood on the sidelines and resisted Churchill's request for involvement in the war. The provisions for neutrality legislation passed by American Congress would not allow the US to sell arms to any country at war and the house and Senate would simply not repeal the law. Lynne Olson does a magnificent job explaining the logic and the quagmire that American politicians were stuck in as they debated and resisted involvement in a war not yet their own.  The people were split into Isolationists and Interventionists: those who advocated fighting only for our own nation and no one else, and those who insisted that America could not evade International responsibility.<br /><br />The author has done a magnificent job bringing alive the time period and the sheer vitriol that people felt for those with opposing points of view regarding involvement in the war. She focuses her attention quite solidly on Lindberg (or perhaps her most interesting chapters are about Lindberg) and his impact and influence on the Isolationists. She depicts Lindberg quite harshly, but perhaps more accurately, compared to many other books about him. His life in Europe, his opinions of the Germans and how he may have been deceived by them regarding their capabilities in aviation are a fascinating part of the book. Lindberg's opinions have far reaching consequences because he is in the national spotlight and Olson explains exactly how his opinions contributed to American lethargy regarding the war. She also does not hesitate to show how he contributed to his own downfall from American Hero to one of the most vilified public figures of the time.<br /><br />There are fascinating characters in this book. Wendell Wilkie, FDR, Churchill, Hoover, etc but I found myself most intrigued by Lindberg and his family. Lindberg's wife, Anne Morrow, easily ignored in all this, has a powerful voice in the book. Lindberg is a fascinatingly flawed human being whose ignorance and stubbornness cause untold harm. There are points in the book where it seems that what Olson should have done is simply written a book about the Lindberg family and their influence on the war!<br /><br />There are certain sections of the book that are quite dry especially since there isn't one particular character or chain of events to follow. There are chapters on Anti Semitism in America, the Yale students who were isolationists, the efforts made by British citizens in America to court support for Britain in the war, etc. For me this is the kind of book that takes a month or two to read since it is a heavy read on its own and is jam packed with historical details and connections that need to be made. What comes through in almost every page is how the issue of entering the war or not managed to split the American populace into two very angry groups; neither of which was willing to see or concede the others' point of view. The nasty atmosphere in the politics aswell as normal life is oddly similar to what we can see even today and the book holds up a mirror to the past as well as to the present.<br /><br />I would highly recommend this book to anyone interested in American History, Charles Lindberg and WWII.well as normal life is oddly similar to what we can see even today and the book holds up a mirror to the past as well as to the present.<br /><br />I would highly recommend this book to anyone interested in American History, Charles Lindberg and WWII.

Special effects are realistic and credible.<br /><br />Aging can be a problem with some geopolitical films; this one is holding up as still reasonably contemporary eight years later. It's not for casual viewing though as there are four related major sub-plots that are advanced simultaneously from very nearly the beginning to the end. The manner in which the film switches between them, and their intellectual depth beyond the typical intelligence agent action genre, requires paying attention to prevent getting lost.<br /><br />Syriana does not tie everything up neatly at the end and in retrospect this is appropriate. It's not to set the stage for a sequel. There is sufficient finality to end the story but maintaining credibility demands it be presented as a time slice on the continuum of fossil fuel energy resources, enormous corporations, and powerful governments. The manner in which Syriana does this should provoke some relevant questions and thinking about the issues afterward.<br /><br />I found it a film well worth seeing, even eight years after its theatrical release, as it's still provocatively relevant. Strongly recommend doing so when attention to the film won't be distracted.

B00CL0J99K|4.0|Let me start by saying that I can understand why there are a lot of split opinions about IRON MAN 3.  It's a film full of misdirection and deceptions, and I was a little distressed by a couple of them - which would give away too much to detail here.  Suffice it to say that there were a couple of moments I felt somewhat cheated; and though I'll be vague about it I can say it involved Mandarin. Still, read on; while I gave the movie four stars, I believe down the road I might very well revise this to a five.  I'll explain...<br /><br />First of all, rest assured there is A LOT of action. And there are a lot of the Iron Suits, too...it's just that Tony Stark isn't often the one IN the suit.  I found myself often frustrated by that fact: Iron Man interruptus, if you will. Either the suits were off on their own, or they were on other people, or they weren't working properly...something that seemed to occur almost constantly.<br /><br />I saw it in Imax 3D, and it was well worth the extra $$ to do so (given the fact that $17 is a RIDICULOUS price for a movie ticket in the first place). The movie was lush, packed with detail, and continuously drew me \\"inside\\" it.  The scenes of (don't panic about spoilers - these are in ALL the promos) Stark's house exploding and falling into the sea are meticulously crafted and extremely inclusive...I really felt the dimensional layers as the house was blown apart: I could feel the force of the detonation, and house collapsing, and the impact of the sea as the house AND Iron Man tumbled into it. Similarly, the moments where his suit fly through the air and \\"find\\" him, or the death-defying scenes aboard (and outside) Air Force One, and so many others throughout the film really do feel like you're there.<br /><br />When it comes to the action you'd expect from a movie such as this, IRON MAN 3 is a rollicking, smash-mouth treat. Director Shane Black - whose only previous directorial role was on [[ASIN:B002QYSZBO Kiss Kiss Bang Bang]] (which ironically served as Robert Downey, Jr.'s comeback film) - does a more-than-capable job, and the characters and events are big and loud. You'll feel like you are on a roller-coaster; while there are of course moments of crazy adrenaline rushes, there are also emotional ups and down.  But Tony Stark is just as glib as always, causing almost as much damage with his mouth as he does as with his armor.  I laughed a lot during the movie ... for those who care, Tony's mechanical manservant JARVIS (the voice of Paul Bettany) gets a good deal of air play here, and he's just as lovably bumbling as always.<br /><br />Yet - and remember my dichotomy about this - the movie really focuses on Tony Stark OUTSIDE the suit. I wish the promos had clued me into this: our man Tony is experiencing Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder because of what happened in [[ASIN:B008NCSZQ8 The Avengers]]. Had I been informed of that I wouldn't have felt slighted, and I think it would have better prepared me for this movie. I found myself constantly disappointed, even cheated, by the absence of Tony WITHIN the suit. I realize now that was the only way I felt Iron man was indeed Iron Man. Yet I will declare that now, looking back, I feel good about the movie, and I'm glad to have seen Tony as a more \\"real\\" person, as a more human man, a man undergoing a metamorphosis.<br /><br />There are other things about this movie that surprised me - and I'm sure will surprise you - that I will NOT tell you. Just realize that while this is a very action-packed movie, it is also a personal one. It's this kind of writing that has typified the action movies of today, making them stand out above their \\"super-hero\\" predecessors. Like the [[ASIN:B001OGWY1W The Dark Knight]] movies of Chris Nolan, these new, and better, powerhouse films are focusing on their heroes' moments of personal growth, warts and all. It's a trend I'm really happy to see.<br /><br />(A NOTE: if you feel negatively about my review, please leave a comment to help me next time.)nt to help me next time.)

B0018O4YSQ|5.0|(NOTE for anyone who wants to give a negative review: please leave a comment to help me do better next time.)<br /><br />POINT 1: This is one TERRIFIC movie!<br />POINT 2: It's not for everyone.<br /><br />One of my top ten movies of all time, this movie is breath-taking from beginning to end. If you're a repeat viewer - and there are many - you might prefer to keep the sound off until Kiefer Sutherland first looks at his watch (it's a tradition among many purists who believe the opening narration gives away too much). But if you're a newcomer, you'll need the opening narration and everything else the movie provides you because, to paraphrase Bette Davis in ALL ABOUT EVE, \\"it's going to be a bumpy ride.\\"<br /><br />DARK CITY is a thrilling head trip from start to finish, a tale that gives you just enough to stand upon and then every so often yanks the rug right out from under you.  But the disorientation is purposeful: director Proyas said that he wanted his viewers to experience the same level of bewilderment that his characters were feeling.<br /><br />We start with a naked man (Rufus Sewell) waking up in a bathtub, the telephone ringing, and when he answers it he's told to leave IMMEDIATELY or THEY will find him there. He has no memory of anything before the phone call, but before he can rush out he finds the body of a woman who has been viciously murdered in one of the rooms. He makes it out just in time for us to see that those who are coming for him virtually float on air. They wear black floor-length leather jackets, and they are known as the Strangers.  THAT at least makes sense, but it's what they can DO, and how it affects the citizens of Dark City, that makes all the difference. It isn't long before we find out the man in the tub is John Murdoch, and HE finds out that not only the strangers want to know what he's doing, but so does a woman who says she's his wife (Jennifer Connelly). And a detective hot on his trail (William Hurt). Oh...and the mouth-breather over the phone, too (Kiefer Sutherland).<br /><br />It's told in a dark noir-ish style, with a nod to the old, clunky cars, clothes, and styles of yesteryear, and the sets seem almost to breathe an atmosphere of tense, claustrophobic fear. And that's not close to the strangest part: at midnight, every night, when the clocks strike the hour, the city grinds to a halt...cars stop, people fall asleep, and the Strangers get busy: dressing citizens in new clothes, placing them in new homes with new memories, and then...the buildings change. Yes, CHANGE. They shrink or grow out of the ground like hungry worms, twisting, widening, thinning, closing off alleys and making no streets. And when the citizens wake they do so without realizing they were out of commission, and life continues on as normal...with new family members, new homes, new jobs...<br /><br />Yes, it's trippy, and a little crazy...but if you have seen and liked [[ASIN:B000HAB4KS The Matrix]], or anything by Jean-Pierre Jeunet ([[ASIN:B004SEUIXS Amelie]], [[ASIN:B000MQ787U The City Of Lost Children]]), or [[ASIN:B001HM1PFO The Rocky Horror Picture Show]], this could be right down your alley. In fact, Richard O'Brien's portrayal of Riff Raff in ROCKY HORROR was the role Proyas credited for inspiring the Strangers.<br /><br />Alex Proyas is an extremely selective director, and in a quarter century of film making he's only made five feature films other than this one.  Three of them bear mentioning: [[ASIN:B005ZC0SYQ The Crow]], [[ASIN:B000I9VZKW I, Robot]] and [[ASIN:B002GGCKYA Knowing]].  While each film at first seems to be quite different from one another (and DARK WORLD), they all display worlds at a crossing.  Within every film there is a moment in which society faces a monumental coming to terms with a supremely metamorphic moment. But at the heart of each movie the metamorphosis occurs because one person stood his ground. And it is that which lies at the heart of this movie - a man who must come to terms with a microverse that makes no sense, where none of the rules apply, and it is only when he understands that - that the rulebook must be thrown out the window - that he begins to effect change.<br /><br />And that, in a nutshell, gives us our insight into this bizarre and wonderful movie...one that the recently late Roger Ebert called the best film of 1998. It was the year before THE MATRIX came out (and if you look hard, you can see the same rooftop scenery - when DARK CITY wrapped, their producers sold its set to the Wachowskis' production company). Certainly, fans of both movies might agree, it's not the only debt THE MATRIX paid to DARK CITY.rms with a microverse that makes no sense, where none of the rules apply, and it is only when he understands that - that the rulebook must be thrown out the window - that he begins to effect change.<br /><br />And that, in a nutshell, gives us our insight into this bizarre and wonderful movie...one that the recently late Roger Ebert called the best film of 1998. 0439023483|5.0|The first -- and by far the best -- of the trilogy.<br /><br />Katniss Everdeen, the heroine, is nothing like the soft, spoiled, boy-crazy teenaged girls who infest the pages of YA; she's a tough, fierce, prematurely grown-up young woman from a hardscrabble West Virginia-like province in what remains of a future United States. Her voice is solid and grounded, believable for who and what she is and refreshingly light on navel-gazing. When she turns introspective, she's usually earned it. The three main men in her world (Gale, her best friend; Peeta, her partner in the Game; and Haymitch, her reprobate coach) are also well-realized, strong characters in their own right.<br /><br />Collins has built a world that, while different from our own, feels authentic and requires only a moderate suspension of disbelief. The Game itself is oddly familiar in this age of reality TV and only a few degrees away from MMA and the other bloodsports we watch today. The characters are engaged by this setting but not overwhelmed by it, as sometimes happens in future-worlds stories.<br /><br />The Hunger Games isn't perfect -- it has a few slow spots, thankfully brief, and Collins is better at descibing one-on-one or two-on-one combat than the two or three large-scale melees that ought to be swirls of kinetic energy (a handicap that especially hurts Mockingjay, the last in the series). Much can be forgiven in the sheer speed of the telling, and in Katniss' often engaging company.<br /><br />Male readers: don't worry that the main character is a girl, because she's a kick-ass girl (no pink cover here). Adults: don't worry that the characters are mostly teenagers. If you're looking for an adventure, there's one right here.

The music is complex, and develops course by course rather than serving you the whole meal in one go. You could almost describe the style as `progressive' in that several numbers evolve from sparse beginnings to increasing multi-layered complexity and in most cases take the listener on an emotional roller-coaster to a satisfying climax.<br /><br />Disc 2 continues in the style of disc 1, with 8 new tracks plus variants on 3 of the disc 1 tracks: `the broken places', `lie down in darkness' and `the day', the theme of which is the day Moby lost his mother to cancer.<br /><br />The DVD includes videos, live-on-stage material, interviews and some odds & sods. There appears to be no `play all' option, so you have to watch the clips one-at-a-time then return to the main menu to make your next selection.<br /><br />The bonus disc sold only with the `deluxe' edition is a live-on-stage concert recorded in London in June 2011. This is Moby at his most energetic onstage with mojo in high gear. Danceable renditions of 14 numbers including `in my heart', `we are all made of stars' `porcelain' and `lift me up' actually better the originally released studio versions.<br /><br />The presentation is fine but understated. In a strong hinged box oozing quality, the 4 discs nest in robust numbered sleeves, and a glossy 12-page booklet full or artistic photo-images completes the package. The whole effect is very tasteful, even highbrow.<br /><br />So, with the deluxe edition of `Destroyed' you get a lot of very fine music for your money.

Pat has been institutionalized because he caught his wife, Nikki, with another man.  And then proceded to beat that man near to death.  Once out, Pat's friend's wife Veronica tries to set him up with Tiffany, an equally-troubled widow who uses dance to cope with her demons.  Figuring he could be charitable, while at the same time convince Nikki to drop the restraining order, Pat decides to help Tiffany with her dance competition, all the while trying to satisfy his father's belief that Pat is the key to a Philadelphia Eagles victory of the Cowboys.<br /><br />When you get right down to it, SILVER LININGS PLAYBOOK is a romantic comedy.  It's not laugh-out-loud funny, and it's not even that romantic, yet that's exactly what the film is.  It's a bit predictable, too, despite writer/director David O. Russell's best efforts.  His previous film, THE FIGHTER, was also a genre piece that more or less stuck to conventions; that film was a superior outing, perhaps due to its grittier subject matter.  PLAYBOOK does try to dig beneath the skin; this is most visible in the scenes between leads Bradley Cooper (who would have won the Oscar if Daniel Day-Lewis hadn't been nominated), and Jennifer Lawrence (who did win the Oscar, even if she deserved it more two years ago for A WINTER'S BONE).  The actors are game for the material; the script starts out strong, bogs down in the middle, then picks up again for a beautifully unconventional dance number (I couldn't take my eyes away from the screen), and throughout the film, these two actors give it their all.  Anyone who only knows Cooper from THE HANGOVER should take note, and anyone who identifies Jennifer Lawrence solely with THE HUNGER GAMES should pull themselves out of puberty for a minute.  (Okay, that's unfair.  The book was actually pretty grown up.)<br /><br />Cooper and Lawrence aren't the only two here, however.  Jacki Weaver, as Pat's mom, is a subtle wonder (and was nominated for Best Supporting Actress, for what it's worth); she doesn't do much, but what she does is terrific.  Julia Stiles is great in her limited role, as is (surprisingly) Chris Tucker.  Shea Wigham continues to get work as Pat's brother; one of these days, he'll be a leading man in his own right, but for now it's nice to see him wherever he pops up.  The true wonder among the supporting cast, however, is Robert De Niro, nominated for Best Supporting Actor.  De Niro isn't as good as he was in his heyday, but this is the best he's been in a long time, and his character is one of the more interesting ones in the film.  His relationship with Pat isn't explored as thoroughly as it should've been, and that is one of the film's many flaws.  SILVER LININGS PLAYBOOK aims high; as such, when it falls flat, one can't help but notice.  But what's here is worth watching, if only for the caliber of performances features.  It may not be a whole lot more than an actor's piece...but hey, there are worse things in the world. This is something inspiring and respectable. But on the other end of the spectrum, it is wise to plan your adventures to some extent to ensure personal safety is a priority. McCandless seemed to have a disregard for his own safety and (somewhat) of a disregard to the awesome power of nature.<br /><br />With that being said, I'm sure McCandless had a deep appreciation and respect for Mother Nature. 1442486937|4.0|According to Holly Black and Tony DiTerlizzi, the text of this story was left for them by three mystery kids.<br /><br />With that stab at blurring the boundaries between fantasy and reality, these two authors slip easily into a haunted house tale that rapidly blossoms into a story of hidden fae and strange creatures. The first of the Spiderwick Chronicles -- \\"The Field Guide\\" -- is a staggering brief story that sets up mysteries, marvels and everything that come after.<br /><br />After their parents' divorce, the Grace kids -- teenage Mallory and nine-year-old twins Jared and Simon -- move with their mother to a crumbling old Victorian house, owned by a weird old aunt. Jared is having a rough time with all of this, but he's distracted from his personal woes by a \\"squirrel\\" scuttling around inside the walls. And their investigations reveal a strange little stash of items that were obviously not taken by a squirrel.<br /><br />An exploratory trip in a dumbwaiter takes Jared to a hidden library full of strange books on mythic creatures -- and soon strange things start happening to the family. Jared's further investigations uncover a strange little book called \\"Arthur Spiderwick's Field Guide To The Fantastical World Around You,\\" a little guide to faery creatures. Jared begins to believe that the unbelievable might be true -- that there are faeries causing trouble in his new home -- but his siblings are a bit more reluctant.<br /><br />For the record, \\"The Field Guide\\" is a short book in a short series full of short books. It's only about a hundred pages of heavily-illustrated text, and we're not talking large pages. It's more like the first chapters of a longer novel -- and it ends on a bit of a cliffhanger.<br /><br />But Holly Black's writing style definitely makes it worth reading. Though the storyline is pretty simple, she weaves a web of subtle, eerie strangeness around the seemingly ordinary circumstances, and builds an increasing sense of dusty, leaf-strewn age around the house. And despite the Victorian house, the sprites and faery creatures here are anything but Victorian -- sometimes ugly, bizarre or malicious (such as when Mallory's hair is tied to her bed).<br /><br />And Tony DiTerlizzi's artwork is simply perfect for the story that he and Black are telling. He crafts lots of intricate pen-and-ink drawings, shadowy depictions of the decayed Victorian mansion, and illustrations of the weirder aspects of the Spiderwick estate (like the dusty library, or the vaguely Satanic door-knocker). The highlights are the old scribbled-on papers of Arthur Spiderwick, including a picture of a household boggart.<br /><br />The main characters are also quite believable: Mallory is a loving but slightly brittle teenager, and the mom is clearly struggling to stay afloat after her divorce. And the twins Jared and Simon (who can be told apart by their hairstyles) make good protagonists for the book, especially since they're so different -- Simon is a tidy animal enthusiast, while the more prominent Jared is a kid who is starting to sink into trouble after his parents' divorce.<br /><br />\\"The Field Guide\\" is a good opening chapter for the Spiderwick Chronicles, building up a sense of suspense, eerie fantasy and otherworldly creatures. Just have the second part on hand before you finish.around the house. 0765337541|5.0|Ender Wiggin is a very unusual boy -- he's a brilliant tactician, a genius, and a despised \\"third\\" in a future that only allows two children. He's also six years old.<br /><br />And despite the fact that Orson Scott Card's sci-fi classic is about a little boy learning how to be a warrior, \\"Ender's Game\\" is a pretty gripping and sometimes grim adventure story. The descriptions of children being taught out how to be cold-blooded warriors is pretty creepy, but the well-developed future world that Card comes up with is pretty awesome.<br /><br />After a fight with a gang of bullies, Ender Wiggin is approached by an army officer who wants him to join the elite Battleschool, where kid geniuses become soldiers -- basically because aliens are about to attack Earth AGAIN and may end up wiping out the human race. His brother Peter is too wild and cruel, and his beloved sister Valentine is too mild-mannered.<br /><br />Ender accepts, and quickly finds himself in a dog-eat-dog space school where he soon becomes loathed for the special treatment the teachers occasionally give him -- when they aren't observing his every move. And it soon becomes obvious that Ender has a natural ability that exceeds that of most of the Battleschool recruits: he instinctively knows how to outmaneuver his opponents and protect himself in a fight, even if he annoys some of the \\"army\\" commanders who don't like being outshone.<br /><br />Back on Earth, his brother and sister try to alter the increasingly unstable politics of Earth by subtle manipulation of the public, a situation that may bring the ruthless Peter into greater power. And as Ender reaches the end of his training, he faces both the buggers and the knowledge of what he is capable of.<br /><br />\\"Ender's Game\\" is kind of an unusual space opera, because the actual war between humans and buggers is not front-and-center until the last act of the story. Up until then, it's about following Ender and his equally unusual siblings as they develop prematurely into adulthood -- these are genius kids who can reshape entire worlds, but you're not really sure that they SHOULD.<br /><br />Card writes in a detailed but brisk style, with pretty realistic dialogue and some ugly dark spots (the description of Peter flaying a squirrel... for no reason). Ender's time at Battle School and Command School feel rather slow at times, only for things to blossom in the final laps of the novel -- Card suddenly turns all our feelings and expectations on their heads. The tragedy of children turned into soldiers becomes even more tragic as we discover what the war is actually all about.<br /><br />As you'd expect from Fascist Hogwarts In Space, there are a lot of kids at Battle School who are developed to certain degrees. But the center of the story is Ender himself -- and despite being a tactical genius with loads of natural ability, Ender never seems like a Wesley Crusher. Like a boy destined to be a Spartan warrior, his inborn skills are what will keep him from ever finding peace. His childhood is sacrificed for war.<br /><br />The other part of the story rests on Peter and Valentine -- Valentine is too nice, while Peter is gradually revealed to be a ruthless genius who works anything and anyone for his goals, which may or may not be self-serving. It's oddly fun to see the growing influence of \\"Demosthenes\\" and \\"Locke\\" on the cruel government.<br /><br />\\"Ender's Game\\" is an intelligent, gripping space opera with an undercurrent of intense tragedy -- it's kind of slow at times, but the strong writing and intriguing main trio keep it afloat.rematurely into adulthood -- these are genius kids who can reshape entire worlds, but you're not really sure that they SHOULD.<br /><br />Card writes in a detailed but brisk style, with pretty realistic dialogue and some ugly dark spots (the description of Peter flaying a squirrel... for no reason). B008XKSW7M|4.0|Quite the cast of characters for this movie- Jennifer Lopez, Elizabeth Banks, Anna Perkins, Chris Rock, Brooklyn Decker, and others.<br /><br />The movie covers the path to pregnancy for several couples. It does a good job demonstrating that the Hollywood, romanticized notion of a \\"perfect pregnancy\\" is possible but only one of several possibilities. Some women will breeze through their 9 months and come out the other end no worse for the wear. For this movie, Brooklyn Decker sneezed and her baby came out. If only all labors were this easy! Elizabeth Banks had a much rougher time, experiencing all of the negatives associated with childbearing.<br /><br />There was also the nontraditional version of a couple deciding to adopt. It was nice they provided some variety, but I was thinking they could have just as easily used a gay or lesbian couple to fill these roles. Perhaps the creators feared some sort of backlash, but it would have been a good place for its use and not changed the story one iota.<br /><br />If you go into this movie hoping for tips on dealing with your pregnancy, good luck. This was more of a reflection on pregnancies in general and the gambit of experiences for these couples. Some surprising topics were covered, such as when Anna Kendrick and her boyfriend lost their baby or the almost death during childbirth. But all in all, it was gently poking fun at the events leading up to childbirth. While not all that funny (it did invoke a few laughs), it is a decent film worth watching once...and that's it.

B00CJ7EK4E|4.0|Rod Stewart has still got it. He has always been a great singer with a uniquely brilliant voice and the skill and musicality to put a song over with real feeling, but some of his choice of material and production style has often made me wonder whether any of the old magic was still there. In places on this album it certainly is. It's variable material, but the good bits are really good.<br /><br />The album opens with a decent but pretty standard pop song, She Makes Me Happy which Rod lifts a bit above the ordinary, and in It's Over it's good to see that he has the dignity to sing about the end of a marriage rather than teenage heartbreak, but he shows that his real greatness is still there in Brighton Beach, a beautiful, deeply evocative song about the recall of first love. It may not be the enduring masterpiece that I Don't Want To Talk About It is, for example, but it affects me nearly as deeply.<br /><br />I won't go through the entire album - there's a good variety of pop, dance and ballads most written by Stewart himself and often reflecting on ageing and times past. Some bits are pretty ordinary, some are very good. I think the three bonus tracks are among the best here, by the way. They're a little more bluesy and a little less produced which gives them a genuineness which I like a lot, and I think they're well worth the extra.<br /><br />The thing that strikes me most strongly is that Rod sounds sincere throughout, probably because he's mainly singing his own songs. He never sounds as though he's going through the motions and even in the places where the production is a bit schmaltzy (which is too often for me, I must say), or the parts when material isn't great, the band sounds fairly ordinary or the lyrics are a bit naff, he sings as though he really means it and manages to lift the whole thing well above the average.<br /><br />Rod Stewart has made some albums which I think would fall into the UN classification of Cruel And Unusual Punishment, so it's good to see that a truly great singer can still make a good record. It certainly isn't up there with Every Picture Tells A Story, but it's not bad at all and has moments of brilliance. Recommendedod to see that a truly great singer can still make a good record. It certainly isn't up there with Every Picture Tells A Story, but it's not bad at all and has moments of brilliance. Recommended

His chieftain fears Ragnar's ambitions, and therein lies a fascinating story of jealousy, intrigue, and power.  The acting, cinematography, and storyline of Vikings are all first-rate.  This story is also historically accurate.  Until the Vikings learned how to navigate and sail the open ocean England was protected from their depredations.  Once that changed England and other European lands were subject to the full \\"fury of the Northmen\\" and a terrible age of slaughter and plunder ensued, which lasted for many centuries.<br /><br />\\"Vikings\\" in no way tries to whitewash the vicious and bloodthirsty nature of the Northmen Vikings, but it does humanize them and give the viewer insight on how things looked from the Viking perspective. B007H1HQW0|3.0|I figured the bad reviews were written by dummies who couldn't read directions or were just clumsy.  My apologies.  The basic concept of this cutter is good.  There's plenty of adjustment capabilities for different size bottles and different lengths of cut.  The one big design flaw is the aluminum bar at the bottom of the jig that parallels the cutter handle.  The angle between the cutter handle and the aluminum bar determines how well the carbide cutter wheel approaches the glass surface.  You need to be able to lock in that angle, but a small screw and wing nut doesn't do the job.  If that angle changes as your pressing and rotating the bottle, the cutter wheel lifts off the glass and ruins the cut, and the bottle.  Also, as you press and rotate the bottle, the cutter slips off center and off the bottle.  Happened to me several times, so I determined that you could only rotate the bottle about 10 degrees before you have to reposition your hand.<br /><br />My success rate is about 50%.  It is critical that the scribed line be perfect, but this jig doesn't allow it consistently.<br /><br />Also, after eight bottles, the first carbide wheel was worn out!  So do the math. You'll only get about about 48 cuts before having to replace the cutter.  Not a big deal, really, but I would expect a carbide wheel to last much longer than that.<br /><br />I'm probably going to build my own jig, one that cradles the bottle in a holder and allows more downward force on the cutter wheel while you rotate the bottle.<br /><br />I'll be returning this item, and I don't recommend it.

B00BSWMITK|4.0|Satriani has assembled a world class ensemble to perform on his latest album.<br /><br />UNSTOPPABLE MOMENTUM - Aptly titled, as Vinnie Colaiuta propels this song from start to finish with a forward momentum akin to that of one of the drummer's strongest influences, Tony Williams.  The song is composed in 5/8 and Colaiuta's penchant for evening out odd-time signatures comes into play, even while he dares to take the piece out of bounds with some of his patented beat displacement and soloing over the final 50 seconds or so...a jazz musician's sense bringing a heavy dosage of sophistication to the simplicity of shred.<br />CAN'T GO BACK - An impeccable groove to accompany tasteful and economical melodies - Satriani's tone and phrasing remind me of Jeff Beck on this one...probably my favorite track on this disc.<br />LIES AND TRUTH - Contains a wonderfully syncopated \\"drums n bass\\" pattern during the verse sections and then briefly breaks into some heavier riffing along the lines of Hendrix's \\"Fire\\" before the chorus blasts through with a straight ahead rock n roll rhythm...an extremely intriguing song throughout.<br />THREE SHEETS TO THE WIND - I was prepared to approach this track as a mere novelty song, but it's a pleasure to hear the band cut loose as the song unfolds - and I can appreciate the 'drunken horn section' brought to the track with the assistance of Keneally's keyboards.<br />I'LL PUT A STONE ON YOUR CAIRN - A brief respite from the fierce and aggressive sounds that surround this interlude. Judging by the title, Satriani intended the piece to have a Gaelic influence - Scottish bagpipes perhaps?<br />A DOOR INTO SUMMER - Straight ahead and mid-tempo rock with catchy commercial melody lines.<br />SHINE ON AMERICAN DREAMER - A simple song composed for the common man...not turning a blind eye to the death of the American Dream, Satriani attempts to express his affinity with the Occupy Wall Street Movement.<br />JUMPIN' IN - Some southern gutter boogie mixedwith a fat back beat, then a new age-y transition, leading to some impressive funk bass by Chaney during the solo section...I really dig this, but perhaps not as much as I am blown away by:<br />JUMPIN' OUT - Colaiuta's half-time shuffle groove is golden on this track and the interplay between the musicians is testimony to the synergy that can occur when great players get together and allow their respective talents to shine.  The hip-hop groove during the breakdown is also a nice touch.<br />THE WEIGHT OF THE WORLD - A bit grandiose at moments, but I like the propulsive energy and especially get a kick out of Keneally's approximation of funk clavinet (ala Don Blackman, Andre Lewis, Stevie Wonder), percolating under a pompous progressive-rock sound.<br />A CELEBRATION - At moments this track recalls some of the grandeur of Eric Johnson's unique style, fusing seemingly disparate elements such as the country shuffle capably dispatched by Colaiuta and the trademark heavy metal proclivities of Satriani.  A fitting conclusion to a strong release packed with such a wide range of diversity.
0307596885|5.0|I've just finished reading Alice Munro's new collection of short stories \\"Dear Life\\" and am, once again, in awe of her prodigious skills in the short story genre.<br /><br />Already in writing this first sentence of my review/reflection, I inadvertently betray how much I have to learn from Munro. It's unlikely that she would use an adjective like \\"prodigious,\\" at least not in one of her compellingly spare stories. Munro writes with a stylistic asceticism that eschews almost all adornment. \\"Eschews\\" is also an improbable word to appear in a Munro piece. (Fortunately, since I didn't have Munro looking over my shoulder as I wrote my novel \\"Searching for Gilead,\\" I had the benefit of skilled editors to pare away superfluous descriptors by which I am all too easily seduced.)<br /><br />Let me open \\"Dear Life\\" and select a page at random. Scanning through the roughly 375-word text on this particular page, I find a total of two adjectives (\\"easy\\" and \\"disgusting\\") and three adverbs (\\"actually,\\" \\"exactly,\\" and \\"primarily.\\")<br /><br />I have two reactions to the lack of literary adornment in Munro's short stories. On the one hand, her capacity to fashion a gripping narrative with the bare minimum of vocabulary is genius. Picturesque canvasses are drawn, the intimacies of characters' inner lives are laid bare, and engaging plot scenarios are unrolled with prosaic everyday language. Reading the selections in \\"Dear Life,\\" I was never bored or distracted. Munro captured me and carried me along from beginning to end.<br /><br />On the other hand, I miss savoring the beautifully poetic descriptions that are typical of other eminent fiction writers. I love luxuriating in the rich prose of a Jane Urquhart, Hilary Mantel, David Mitchell, Esi Edujyan, or Allan Hollinghurst, let alone the classics of a Marcel Proust or Charles Dickens or Jane Austen. I'm thrilled by the writings of those who can draw from the teaming resource bank of languages to create powerful narratives that enthrall me and elegant descriptions that take my breath away.<br /><br />With that (not inconsequential) caveat, let me return to celebrate one other aspect of Alice Munro's writing. In \\"Dear Life,\\" which may possibly be the final book that is published by this Canadian literary giant, Munro demonstrates once again her capacity to create a wide variety of credible and internally consistent voices.  Within a paragraph or two of the beginning of each new story, the reader has a clear sense of the narrator or visual of the protagonist. And each voice, in each story, is unique.<br /><br />Almost. The last four stories in \\"Dear Life\\" have autobiographical elements. As Munro says of these four, \\"I believe they are the first and last--and the closest--things I have to say about my own life.\\" They are absolute gems and are all focused around the life of a young girl, presumably the same young girl who is, apparently, Alice Munro. My favourite, if I were forced to choose, is \\"Voices\\" in which a pre-pubescent girl is witness from a very great distance to the traumatic reverberations of war. The subtlety of the story is awesome--we readers discern what is going through the child's narration while the girl herself is left perplexed by what she is seeing and hearing.<br /><br />My novel \\"Searching for Gilead\\" went through five drafts based on feedback that I received from friends whose judgment I valued and two editors who were sympathetic yet ruthless in their advice. The published book is immeasurably improved from the initial versions largely as a result of a protracted exorcising of unnecessary and distracting verbiage (I know--I hear you readers, writers and editors decrying the redundancy of the phrase \\"unnecessary and distracting verbiage.\\")  I am now at work on a new writing project--a collection of inter-related short stories with the working title \\"Booktales.\\" Hopefully, having read and learned from Alice Munro's \\"Dear Life,\\" I am further ahead than when I started work on \\"Searching for Gilead.\\"<br /><br />* * *<br />For more information on Alice Munro's \\"Dear Life\\", see: [...]<br /><br />For information on my novel \\"Searching for Gilead\\" and my memoir \\"August Farewell\\", see my website at [...]Searching for Gilead.\\"<br /><br />* * *<br />For more information on Alice Munro's \\"Dear Life\\", see: [...]<br /><br />For information on my novel \\"Searching for Gilead\\" and my memoir \\"August Farewell\\", see my website at [...]

B008AUGEFC|5.0|What was going on in David Mitchell's head while he was writing his stunning novel \\"Cloud Atlas\\"?<br /><br />Brain scientists could probably have mapped the electrical and chemical activity. His wife would have been able to detail his distractedness from the day-to-day demands of family life in their home in Ireland.<br /><br />None of that technical or domestic information would give us much of a clue about how Mitchell conceived of a 19th century notary by the name of Adam Ewing writing a journal in the Pacific that gets discovered in 1931 by a frustrated composer Robert Frobisher working in Belgium who writes letters to his friend (and apparent lover) Rufus Sixsmith who becomes involved in a 1975 case of corruption involving a California nuclear power plant which is investigated by journalist Luisa Rey and then turned into a novel that falls into the hands in the early 21st century of a British publisher Timothy Cavendish imprisoned against his will in an eccentric nursing home a movie of which is watched by Somni~451 a futuristic clone rebelling against her totalitarian state which itself becomes the subject of a holographic projection viewed by primitive tribesman Zachry in a far off post-apocalyptic future which turns out to be the apex of the novel that then rewinds chronologically revisiting and picking up the story from where it left off earlier involving Somni~451, Timothy Cavendish, Luisa Rey, Rufus Sixsmith, Robert Frobisher, and Adam Ewing.<br /><br />Are you still with me?<br /><br />David Mitchell's imagination somehow came up with this gargantuan story line and then committed it to prose that ranges from lyrically beautiful phrasing to ancient and futuristic dialects that, I presume, are at least in part of Mitchell's creation.<br /><br />I'm in awe of the miracle of the imagination, not just the exemplary case-in-point of David Mitchell's, but of all creative artists.<br /><br />I've had some first-hand experience watching the imagination at work and am as confounded by the process close-up as I am when appreciating the product of someone else's mind.<br /><br />I've recently written a memoir \\"August Farewell\\" and a novel \\"Searching for Gilead.\\" The memoir is based on my life with and the death of my long-term gay partner and hence is biographical. But how did I conceive of the structure of integrating vignettes of our thirty-three-year relationship into the day-by-day chronology of the two weeks between his cancer diagnosis and his death? The novel writing is even more of a mystery to me. I knew the general storyline that I wanted to produce but when I would begin a new chapter I often had no idea how it would progress. Like an out-of-body experience, I watched the characters take on a life of their own as they interacted, argued, made love, conspired against one another, and reconciled. It was all coming out of my head, my imagination, but how it evolved seemed only partially a matter of my conscious will and very largely a volatile creative force for which I felt I was not much more than the channel.<br /><br />Thank goodness for the imagination - one of the miracles of life.<br /><br />See it on full display in David Mitchell's \\"Cloud Atlas\\".<br /><br />* * *<br />For information on David Mitchell's \\"Cloud Atlas\\", see: [...]<br /><br />For information on my memoir \\"August Farewell\\" and my novel \\"Searching for Gilead\\" including short YouTube book trailers, see my website at [...]am when appreciating the product of someone else's mind. 0061692034|5.0|As my partner Bill lay in bed in our home dying of pancreatic cancer, he told me that there was one message that he wanted me to say to his family members and friends during his funeral service: \\"Tell them to be kind to one another.\\"<br /><br />I was reminded of this as I read the final page in Richard Ford's recent novel \\"Canada.\\"<br /><br />It's not that Bill's words and those that Ford has his narrator speak were conveying the same message. Rather, it is that deceptively simple injunctions to others or observations on life or reflections about human nature can carry a wallop far out of proportion to their seeming innocuousness.<br /><br />Richard Ford has a capacity to communicate profound philosophical insights through casual comments that his characters drop in mostly unselfconscious moments. \\"Canada\\" is teeming with them as my highlighting pen gives witness. A few examples drawn randomly:<br />- \\"... it's the edging closer to the point of no return that's fascinating...\\"<br />- \\"It's been my habit of mind, over the years, to understand that every situation in which human beings are involved can be turned on it's head...knowing this, however, has not made me cynical. Cynical means believing that good isn't possible; and I know for a fact that good is.\\"<br />- \\"...if anyone's mission in the world was to gain experience, it might be necessary, as I'd already thought, to become someone different--even if I didn't know who.\\"<br />- \\"Which may finally be the only real difference between one place on the earth and another: how you feel about the people, and the difference it makes to you to think that way.\\"<br />- \\"What I know is, you have a better chance in life--of surviving it--if you tolerate loss well.\\"<br /><br />Grouped together like this might give a sense that Ford's \\"Canada\\" is a ponderous read and yet such personally philosophical and, one might say, existential reflections never obstruct the quietly paced narration of the story. I read the novel more slowly than I read most fiction, in part because I was relishing Ford's writing, and in part because I was trying to ferret out how Ford is able to convey so much of the internal mindscape of his narrator without succumbing to the tedious descriptions of \\"I'm-feeling-this-now\\" and \\"now-I'm-feeling-that\\" which make much contemporary fiction boring.<br /><br />I think that I've come up with a few clues. Ford is so skilled in character depiction that he is able to create a credible narrator who can share details of his own thoughts and feelings without coming off as either exhibitionistic or self-absorbed. Secondly, the philosophical reflections emerge organically from the story line. And thirdly, the narrator's observations strike us as simultaneously novel and familiar--we are jarred by the uniqueness of the insight but then immediately recognize how they resonate with our own perceptions of the world around us and within us.<br /><br />Which is, after all, why so many of us drawn to reading and writing fiction--to help others and ourselves draw meaning out of the life that we experience and witness. Richard Ford, in my view, is a master at writing philosophy in the genre of the novel.<br /><br /> * * *<br /><br />For information on Richard Ford's novel \\"Canada,\\" see: [...]<br /><br />For information on my memoir \\"August Farewell\\" and my novel \\"Searching for Gilead\\", see my website at: [...]ly than I read most fiction, in part because I was relishing Ford's writing, and in part because I was trying to ferret out how Ford is able to convey so much of the internal mindscape of his narrator without succumbing to the tedious descriptions of \\"I'm-feeling-this-now\\" and \\"now-I'm-feeling-that\\" which make much contemporary fiction boring.<br /><br />I think that I've come up with a few clues. B007XU0N1G|5.0|So, this is my 1st review ever. Thought I'd give a little history first. After exploring the entire Rhapsody of Fire discography, they have become one of my favorite bands ever, some of their best works being Power of the Dragonflame( or pretty much anything from The Emerald Sword Saga ) and Frozen Tears of Angels, IMO ( although all of their albums are amazing ). Been listening to them for a few years now and there are very few bands out there than match the epicness and virtuosity of RoF. A bit fanboyish, I'll admit but anyways, moving on. I'll admit I was a bit nervous when I heard Luca left to form his own version of the band, now known as Luca Turilli's Rhapsody. Seen as a fresh new start for Luca Turilli so he can fufull his musical visions, he most certainly delivers here, delivering some of his best work to date. I've given this album a few spins already and am loving it, and now that I've had some time to let it settle in, I'd put it on the same level as the albums I mentioned above.  So how would I describe this album? Well if you're at all familiar with RoF, then you already know what to expect. You've got the neoclassical melodic guitar solos and speedy riffs by Luca, as he delivers some of his best work to date. You've got the latin choirs and the big orchestral sounds, so it doesn't stray too far from the typical Rhapsody sound. But there are a few twists throughout the album, there are a few new things, like some electronic sounds throughout the album and as heard on Quantum X, almost sounds like techno. And on the song Dante's Inferno, there's a part that sounds kind of alien. I cant quite explain it, but it sounds space-y. The beginning of Clash of the Titans has some police sirens at the beginning. Overall it's the same Rhapsody, with a few new tricks. Also, the narration returns on this album, and it sounds like the narration to a movie trailer or something like that( of course this is coming from Luca's passion for cinematic scores) but thankfully he struck a good balance with it and kept to a minimum. My biggest worry was who could Luca find to sing for the band. Fabio Lione ( vocalist in RoF ) is one of the best vocalists in power metal, so those are some big shoes to fill. Thankfully, Luca hired Alessandro Conti and he is a damn good vocalist, plain and simple. His vocal style fits like a glove. He reminds me a lot of Fabio and can hit those high, soaring vocals no problem. As for the songs themselves, they are really good. Rather than explain each song, I'll just say they mostly fall into fast tempo songs with lots of solos with a few slower songs in between. I found myself listening to the whole album from start to finish and just getting lost in the music. If I had to pick the absolute best songs, I'd have to say Excalibur, Tormento E Passione, Dark Fate of Atlantis ( a fast and heavy song ), their cover of Helloween's March of Time, and the 16-minute epic, Of Michael the Archangel and Lucifer's Fall. Really, the whole album is fantastic, but if I had to pick one weak link, it's the song Luna. Not necessarily a bad song, but it seems a bit out of place having a pop-ish ballad in the middle of the maelstrom of fast solos and speedy riffs. It doesnt stand out like the rest of the songs on here. Other than that, this is an excellent album and it'll be in rotation for a long time. Can't wait to see what Luca will come up for the next one and to see what RoF will come up with, the bar has been set pretty high. Check out some of those songs and if you like them, you should pick this album up, these guys really deserve the support. If your into symphonic power metal/ progressive metal, definitely pick this one up. I give this one an excellent 9/10, or 4.5 stars.

0061950726|5.0|During my Sunday morning commute to church, I had the good fortune to hear a wonderful interview on NPR Radio of author Christina Kline and the discussion of her new book, &#34;Orphan Train.&#34;  I was astonished that I had never heard this account of &#34;Orphan Trains&#34; as part of our nations history.  The story is based on the author's extensive research of train orphans that were unwanted children from east coast cities, especially New York City.  The &#34;Orphan Train&#34; operation began in 1854 until around 1929 during the Great Depression in relocating children to areas in the mid-west where they worked on farms, did child labor or did extensive work at foster homes caring for many children.  The story evolves around two orphans; one, Vivian Daly, an Irish immigrant and Molly Ayer. a Pentoscot Indian.  Both of these women spent difficult times in foster care and this author brings you closely into their lives.  You feel every struggle that they endure and every change in their lives.  This story will tug at your heart; while you may forget many other stories of fiction, you will always remember the children of the orphan train, especially Vivian and Molly.  When you reach the last page of &#34;Orphan Train,&#34; you will feel it has been an great reading experience. This is a story of fiction but reflects, in a similar manner, lives of unfortunate children.  This would be a great book for book discussion.  My only complaint was with the publisher's choice of very small font size and poor book binding quality.  This book is a literary treasure and it should have been published in a quality that it deserves. So the publisher gets a &#34;F&#34; and the author gets 5 stars.  Thank you Christina Baker Kline - a great read!

B003UV8TFE|5.0|It is widely assumed that a good scientist is also good at teaching. At the same time the stereotypical scientist is reclusive and hidden behind piles of paper covered in chalk dust. Needless to say that all of these are completely unrelated - and along comes Walter Lewin.<br /><br />\\"For the Love of Physics\\" is not a book about everything you ever wanted to know nor a comprehensive review of our fundamental theories of the universe. It is an exhibition of raw curiosity that every one of us probably has felt at one or another point in our lives. It is that original spark which is at the core of a good scientist. The boldness to ask questions without the fear of not understanding the answer. Even better, the answers are beautifully simple once the relations are laid out (in principle). Here is the scientist who can't help himself but go out and let other people rediscover the world around them.<br /><br />\\"For the Love of Physics\\" is in part biography and in fact starts with a chapter on the author's childhood, although these parts never serve their own purpose. There may be short chalk board type excursions (no equations, though) but in the end everything always leads back to the basic motivation. Research happens in all our lives and whatever we do in research needs to be understandable (or maybe adorable) by everyone. That being said, this book is for everyone. If you ever felt that certain curiosity about nature and the universe, here is your guide to find it again. If not... find it for the first time.

For the usual christian they may see the film as a statement that equates the wages of sin amounting to utter ruin. For there is a point that can be made that to the conservatives Larry Flints life was one of literal implosion. Living with no limits or control leads a slippery slop to demise. To the christian this film is an example that drives home the common statement The wages of sin is death. In the case of Flint death follows through excess, drug use, loss of control and mental deterioration. Indeed most of us wouldnt want to trade places with Flint no matter how much money was involved. His life indeed was a tragic one. To some however Flint is seen as something of a hero. A voice for free speech and a free america. Flint in reality was a man with clearly no moral convictions at all, be it spiritual or secular. Without some conviction to rule your life in one form or another total misdirection and demise follow. This film in my opinion never judges the religious order or the heathen one. It masterfully stands by as an observer and simply tells the story as it really happened. Leaving each individual to decide which side in this debate they would really want to be part of. In the midst of this are three incredible performances by Woody Harrelson, Cortney Love, and Edward Norton. The film is often hysterically funny then sadly heartbreaking, and sometimes even operatic in its creativity.

The author reverses herself on so many things she wrote previously;a last minute \\"fix\\" for Sookie's telepahty problem when she firmly established in earlier books (see conversations with Niall & Mr Catiliades) that it couldn't be taken away. I just wish that in the end she had cared about them as much as her readers do.

0393330435|1.0|Growing up on the Apple //e, Woz has always been a bit of a personal hero to me. Throughout watching the story of Apple unfold, I have always looked at Steve Jobs as the unsympathetic, opportunistic viper who basically used Woz and threw him away as soon as he had outlived his usefulness. When I finally received a copy of this book I devoured it quickly and with each passing page my disappointment grew. This book is deficient in so many ways that it is truly unforgivable. It's so bad that it has actually turned me on my ear a bit regarding Jobs - I now very much want to read his book, because I wonder whether putting up with Woz' passive-aggressive anti-humility drove him as crazy as it did me... and I just read a book Woz wrote, nevermind having to work with him day in and day out for years on end!<br /><br />First of all Woz, I would have thought you'd have figured that anyone who was interested in reading your life story might be at least in some small part technically minded and that you might have given consideration, for five freaking seconds, that we might be bored to tears or even borderline INSULTED by a book written at a fifth grade reading level. Seriously, this stupid book reads like Wikipedia's simple English version. A side effect of this editorial choice is that anything CLOSE to being technical concerning the development of the Apple ][ (which would have been incredibly interesting to this reader) is largely glossed over or ignored altogether. The most exciting part of the book for me was reading about the development of the floppy 5.25 - a triumph and testimonial to just how brilliant Woz is - yet even this was simplified to a maddening degree and followed by a profuse apology for 'getting so technical.' YOU DIDN'T GET TECHNICAL!<br /><br />Then there is that maddening anti-humility. Woz, anyone who picks up your book already knows you're a genius. It's part of why we love you and it's part of why we want to read your story. So why wouldn't you just tell your story and let it speak for itself? I simply can't understand the decision to push the story AT the reader rather than simply telling the story and letting the reader make his or her own inferences. This is a difficult thing to put into words as a reviewer and to demonstrate fully, but reading the book you get the feeling that Woz feels underappreciated by anyone who might read it. Every sentence feels as if he is screaming out for you to appreciate him while simultaneously being embarrassed for having to ask and subsequently tempering his assertions with \\"aw shucks\\" nonsense. \\"The Apple ][ was the computer that changed the world. So they tell me.\\" OH COME ON.<br /><br />The practical upshot of Woz' decision to write the book this way is that he comes off (inadvertently, I think) as a guy who is completely full of himself and who thinks everyone in his potential audience are borderline idiots who could never understand him, his thought processes or his motivations. The plane crash part of the book changed my mind about this though. This is the only place where the book finally started to make a little sense to me. Woz tries to gloss over this event too but I was left wondering just how much that crash messed up his mind. It certainly seems like the kind of thing that can send a genius off into mad pastures and effectively end his career - culminating in the writing of an overly-simplistic book like this one. Ultimately, I left thinking that this must be exactly what happened.tell your story and let it speak for itself? 0385349947|4.0|There is a lot in this book. It is part anecdote and memoir, part business class, part psychological analysis, part cultural analysis and, most importantly, full of footnotes. The footnotes are there because, quite frankly, some of the statistics cited are impressive and without a citation it would be easy to dismiss them as exaggeration.<br /><br />This book puts in black and white a lot of facts about being a woman in business and technology.<br /><br />There's also a lot of gray and the lessons to be gleaned are not exclusive to women. Sandberg does an excellent job of working her way through her critical mistakes as well her perceived mistakes that turned out to be gold mines.<br /><br />A section that resonated with me was in the middle of the book when she talks about crying at work. I too am a person prone to tears when I'm sufficiently angry or betrayed. Sandberg tells two stories of how her tears actually forged stronger connections with the men she was working for. She goes on to describe how she evolved a style that connected more deeply with colleagues rather than just being all business.<br /><br />When I see these best-case outcomes, I'm encouraged, but I'm not foolish enough to believe it will work every time. But it does make it easier to analyze a situation, make a better choice and move on. Even within the same company, no two managers will necessarily react the same way to person's request for help.<br /><br />Ultimately, there were countless examples of the unconscious short-changing we tend to do to ourselves. \\"Women rarely make one big decision to leave the workforce. Instead, they make a lot of small decisions along the way, making accommodations and sacrifices that they believe are required to have a family someday. Of all the ways women hold themselves back, perhaps the most pervasive is that they leave before they leave.\\" Does that sound harsh? Absolutely. Is it true when you start thinking about the little things and small choices? 1400069807|5.0|This 480 page book is the result of 3.5 years of investigative journalism by Pulitzer Prize winner Michael Moss. This is a serious book with many notes and citations to back up the statements made within. I either knew or suspected every single thing in this book but I still learned a number of things and had my suspicions confirmed.<br /><br />I appreciated the professional tone of the book and love the fact that it was not a soapbox rant or unfounded opinions being slung around without just cause. The only time he strays into opinion is with some anti-Wall Street bias in two places in the book; the food companies are in the business to drive profits and when they choose to go public the investors are the owners, so what's wrong with wanting profits or increased sales? In the final three paragraphs in the epilogue he finally reveals why he wrote the book: to inform the consumer and to empower them to make food and drink consumption choices that are best for them.<br /><br />Moss documents interviews with food scientists and marketing and advertising professionals. He tells the stories of food company's beginnings and about processed food successes and failures.<br /><br />Of vital importance for Moss's expose is the explanation of how the processed food industry has spent lots of R&D money to show the effect of food on the brain: what things can we become addicted to (spawning eating more in between meals and to eat those things during meals), what components cause cravings (to, and which foods impede satiety therefore causing us to overeat at each meal. We know and accept that illegal drugs and alcohol and cigarettes or caffeine in coffee and other drinks can affect our brain, but seldom do we hear about how common foods in the Standard American Diet act as drugs in our brain: causing addiction, triggering cravings, and leading to over-consumption.<br /><br />The book is in three sections, in a different order than in the title; I later figured out why. Sugar goes first to establish the effect on the brain and the addiction and cravings it creates and sugar is an energy food for humans. It has a calculable bliss point to help recipe creators match the food to what will make the brain happy. Fat is next, and it is also an energy food for humans, and it has a high bliss point, beyond what the body needs for energy to survive. Salt is last in the book and it brings out and amplifies flavors. Although is not essential for human life to survive it is easy to become addicted to and the tolerance builds and more and more is needed to satisfy. At the end it comes full circle to explain how those three things are key to potentially harmful to humans if not eaten in moderation and how all three are essential to producing processed foods that can be cooked or baked to a nice appearing food that has the intended flavor after a sometimes long shelf life.<br /><br />I had previously learned about the addictive nature of sugar in books by Kathleen Desmartins (Potatoes not Prozac and The Sugar Addict's Total Recovery Program).  I had previously learned about the addictive nature of sugar in books by Kathleen Desmartins ([[ASIN:141655615X Potatoes Not Prozac: Solutions for Sugar Sensitivity]]and [[ASIN:0345441338 The Sugar Addict's Total Recovery Program]]).<br /><br />Interwoven through the book is an explanation of the US government's role with the Department of Agriculture and the FDA and how they are looking out more for the food companies than for the health of American consumers whose taxes pay their salaries.<br /><br />This book focuses on processed foods. I am disappointed that Moss mentioned that newborns love sugar and hate salt and that formula companies are trying to make formula sweeter. I was disappointed that Moss never mentioned human milk (breast milk) and told us what the sweetness level exactly is (it is high). I like to have heard something about breastfeeding vs. formula feeding and about the controversial marketing strategies of formula companies, only so it could reach millions. If you want that info, read Gabrielle Palmer's [[ASIN:190517716X The Politics of Breastfeeding: When Breasts are Bad for Business]] and in [[ASIN:031336060X Milk, Money, and Madness: The Culture and Politics of Breastfeeding]]by Naomi Baumslag as well as WHO policies on artificial baby milk; those are issues that started with Nestle, one company profiled in the book.<br /><br />Moss does not attack wheat or grains (see Wheat Belly) or cow milk (see Don't Drink Your Milk by Frank Osci MD or [[ASIN:0761501312 Raising Your Child Without Milk: Reassuring Advice and Recipes for Parents of Lactose-Intolerant and Milk- Allergic Children]] by Jane Zukin.) He also does not discuss unfermented soy (what is eaten in the US), see [[ASIN:0967089751 The Whole Soy Story: The Dark Side of America's Favorite Health Food]] by Kaayla Daniel. If you want to learn about corn, see the documentary [[ASIN:B0047N482M King Corn [HD]]]. For updated info on meat (instead of the old book [[ASIN:0486419231 The Jungle (Dover Thrift Editions)]]), see the film [[ASIN:1586486942 Food Inc.: A Participant Guide: How Industrial Food is Making Us Sicker, Fatter, and Poorer-And What You Can Do About It]].<br /><br />Although some mothers discuss how food can cause negative moods or bad behaviors in young children, Moss does not go there (see [[ASIN:0688119077 Is This Your Child?]]). He doesn't get into what foods can mimic ADHD or get into food intolerances or<br /><br />Moss chooses to stick to big diseases well accepted in western medicine professionals: high blood pressure, heart disease, diabetes, and obesity. He mentions childhood obesity and the growing rate of childhood diabetes and new signs of increased heart disease in children.<br /><br />I read the book cover to cover and learned some new things and it solidified some of what I already knew. I have been reading about food and health for 25 years (and have read all but one book I recommended here). I have tried to live a life of wellness, mostly relying on eating whole foods and foods slow cooked or baked from scratch. Although Moss was preaching to the choir with me and I am glad I read it.<br /><br />I rate this book 5 stars = I Love It because it is a solid piece of investigative journalism (even though it didn't touch on every single issue that exists).ategies of formula companies, only so it could reach millions. B00AS1Q8FW|3.0|Earlier in 2012 and for whatever reason, Tom Cruise decided a singing role in The Rock of Ages would be a smart role for his borderline flailing career.  Hopefully, Cruise learned a lesson that an un-relatable character, like Stacee Jaxx, was always going to be pitiful to watch, even if he did star in the massively successful Mission: Impossible sequel just a few months prior.  What's the point here?  A typical Tom Cruise film is either incredibly good or horribly bad.  Despite his reputation outside the world of film, Cruise is still a reputable actor - and if he'd stick to a certain genre of film, he might even be able to revitalize his career to its previous prominence.  Clearly, he's at his best in action flicks, and even might be able to sell himself in a comedy role (Tropic Thunder), but outside of those roles, it's difficult to take him seriously right now.  Sure, within the last ten years he's starred in a few intriguing dramas, like The Last Samurai or Collateral - but since Collateral (despite his box office success stories), Cruise has gone stale.  Could Jack Reacher be a career difference maker?<br /><br />Directed by Christopher McQuarrie (The Way of the Gun), Jack Reacher stars Tom Cruise as a former military police officer, named Jack Reacher.  Now a drifter, Reacher arrives in Pittsburgh after former military sniper, James Barr (Joseph Sikora) allegedly kills five people outside a sports stadium - someone Reacher has already investigated in the past.  However, as much as Reacher would like Barr to be guilty, he discovers not everything is as it seems and takes it upon himself to investigate these murders that the District Attorney, Alex Rodin (Richard Jenkins) believes are worthy of the death penalty.  At the same time, Rodin's daughter and defense attorney, Helen Rodin (Rosamund Pike), agrees to defend Barr and assist Reacher to try to save Barr from the death penalty.  Robert Duvall, David Oyelowo, Werner Herzog, and Jai Courtney also star in supporting roles.<br /><br />Much like the Jason Bourne character for the Bourne novels, Jack Reacher also originates from a series of novels - created by author Lee Child.  Jason Bourne and Jack Reacher are very similar characters, not so much because of their origin (even though they are both officers), but because they are two incredibly powerful individuals (mentally and physically) that you wouldn't really want to mess with.  Unfortunately, unless you're digging into their past, characters of this nature are only interesting to an extent.  Jack Reacher is well-trained in combat and weaponry, so yeah - he's a total badass.  But, what fun is it to watch one guy constantly succeed at everything?  He takes down five guys with no problem (not to mention he does it with a Spider-Man-like banter), hits a bullseye with a sniper rifle like it's no big deal, and is basically smarter than everyone else (especially the police and a District Attorney).  As an introduction to the Jack Reacher character, this film does a fine job of putting him on the map.  In future films, though, he needs to be more flawed, a bit more open to emotion, and have a better villain trying to kill him - otherwise nothing sets him apart from established characters, like Jason Bourne, James Bond, Agent 47 (Hitman), or Rambo.<br /><br />As far as Tom Cruise goes, he does his usual \\"thing\\" with the starring role in Jack Reacher.  The description of the Reacher character in the novels is a tall, overpowering man.  Obviously, Cruise doesn't really fit that description - but it doesn't hurt the film that he's not the perfect character actor for Jack Reacher.  Cruise is easily believable as this smart, athletic drifter that mysteriously outshines everyone else around him.  He's not an ego driven character, yet he's driven none-the-less.  In any event, the spotlight is on Tom Cruise for the duration of the film, briefly allowing newcomer Jai Courtney to spread his wings as the villainous henchman, Charlie.  Speaking of Charlie, his boss, a Russian businessman known only as The Zec, is supposed to be the \\"big bad\\" of the film, yet the character is weakly written and without an overpowering motive for his villainy.<br /><br />The underlying question is:  is Jack Reacher worth watching?  The quick answer is yes, but it comes with strings attached.  If you combined films, like Shooter, Rambo, or any other \\"frame job\\" film, you can already say you've seen Jack Reacher.  However, you can make that quick judgment about most every film these days.  Jack Reacher is a new, mysterious character that walks around with a great deal of baggage - and he's an honest, charismatic character with personal qualities to be admired.  In the end, this film creates more questions than it provides answers, which means there better be a few more sequels, otherwise this film will fail to stand apart from the pre-mentioned titles in the future.<br /><br />Overall Film Grade:  C<br />FNDNreview.com<br />Facebook.com/FNDNReview<br />Twitter.com/FriNiteDateNiteeaking of Charlie, his boss, a Russian businessman known only as The Zec, is supposed to be the \\"big bad\\" of the film, yet the character is weakly written and without an overpowering motive for his villainy.<br /><br />The underlying question is:  is Jack Reacher worth watching?  The quick answer is yes, but it comes with strings attached.  If you combined films, like Shooter, Rambo, or any other \\"frame job\\" film, you can already say you've seen Jack Reacher.  However, you can make that quick judgment about most every film these days.  Jack Reacher is a new, mysterious character that walks around with a great deal of baggage - and he's an honest, charismatic character with personal qualities to be admired.  In the end, this film creates more questions than it provides answers, which means there better be a few more sequels, otherwise this film will fail to stand apart from the pre-mentioned titles in the future.<br /><br />Overall Film Grade:  C<br />FNDNreview.com<br />Facebook.com/FNDNReview<br />Twitter.com/FriNiteDateNite
